1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<ul><li class="mw-empty-elt"></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Wales_(1959%E2%80%93present).svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of Wales (1959\xe2\x80\x93present).svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg/16px-Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png" width="16" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg/32px-Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="830" data-file-height="498" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Wales" title="Portal:Wales">Wales  &#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Culture_of_Wales" title="Template:Culture of Wales"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Culture_of_Wales" title="Template talk:Culture of Wales"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Welsh</b> (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Cymraeg</i>; <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Welsh" title="Help:IPA/Welsh">[k\xc9\x99m\xcb\x88rai\xc9\xa1]</a></span><small class="nowrap">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Cymraeg.wav" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/97/Cymraeg.wav" class="internal" title="Cymraeg.wav">listen</a></span></span>)</small>) or <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">y Gymraeg</i> (<small>Welsh pronunciation:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Welsh" title="Help:IPA/Welsh">[\xc9\x99 \xc9\xa1\xc9\x99m\xcb\x88rai\xc9\xa1]</a></span>) is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">Brittonic</a> branch of the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a>. It is spoken natively in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, by some in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, and in <i><a href="/wiki/Y_Wladfa" title="Y Wladfa">Y Wladfa</a></i> (the Welsh colony in <a href="/wiki/Chubut_Province" title="Chubut Province">Chubut Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Historically, it has also been known in English as "Cambrian",<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> "Cambric"<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and "Cymric".<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Of usual residents in Wales aged three and over, 19.0% were able to speak Welsh according to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">United Kingdom Census 2011</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2001">2001 Census</a>, 20.8 per cent of the population aged 3+ were able to speak Welsh. This suggests that there was a decrease in the number of Welsh speakers in Wales from 2001 to 2011 \xe2\x80\x93 from about 582,000 to 562,000 respectively. However, according to the Welsh Language Use Survey 2013\xe2\x80\x9315, 24 per cent of people aged three and over living in Wales were able to speak Welsh, implying a possible increase in the prevalence of the Welsh language since the 2011 census.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Language_Commissioner" title="Welsh Language Commissioner">Welsh Language (Wales) Measure 2011</a> gave the Welsh language official status in Wales,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> making it the only language that is <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> official in any part of the United Kingdom, with English being <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> official. The Welsh language, along with English, is also a <i>de jure</i> official language of the National Assembly for Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Welsh_language" title="History of the Welsh language">History of the Welsh language</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Still_surviving..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_406078.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Still_surviving..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_406078.jpg/220px-Still_surviving..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_406078.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Still_surviving..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_406078.jpg/330px-Still_surviving..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_406078.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Still_surviving..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_406078.jpg/440px-Still_surviving..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_406078.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Still_surviving..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_406078.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This tattered <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Welsh" title="Bible translations into Welsh">Welsh Bible</a> of 1620, in <a href="/wiki/Llanwnda,_Pembrokeshire" title="Llanwnda, Pembrokeshire">Llanwnda</a> church, was rescued from the hands of French invaders in 1797.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The language of the Welsh developed from the language of <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">Britons</a>, according to academic <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Charles-Edwards" title="Thomas Charles-Edwards">T. M. Charles-Edwards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The emergence of Welsh was not instantaneous and clearly identifiable. Instead, the shift occurred over a long period of time, with some historians claiming that it had happened by as late as the 9th century, with a watershed moment being that proposed by Kenneth H. Jackson, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Deorham" title="Battle of Deorham">Battle of Dyrham</a>, a military battle between the West Saxons and the Britons in 577 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-koch_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-koch-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>, which split the South Western British from direct overland contact with the Welsh.\n</p><p>Four periods are identified in the history of Welsh, with rather indistinct boundaries: Primitive Welsh, Old Welsh, Middle Welsh, and Modern Welsh. The period immediately following the language\'s emergence is sometimes referred to as Primitive Welsh,<sup id="cite_ref-koch_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-koch-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> followed by the <a href="/wiki/Old_Welsh" title="Old Welsh">Old Welsh</a> period \xe2\x80\x93 which is generally considered to stretch from the beginning of the 9th century to sometime during the 12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-koch_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-koch-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Middle_Welsh" title="Middle Welsh">Middle Welsh</a> period is considered to have lasted from then until the 14th century, when the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Welsh_language" title="History of the Welsh language">Modern Welsh</a> period began, which in turn is divided into Early and Late Modern Welsh.\n</p><p>The name <i>Welsh</i> originated as an <a href="/wiki/Exonym_and_endonym" title="Exonym and endonym">exonym</a> given to its speakers by the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>, meaning "foreign speech" (see <a href="/wiki/Walhaz" title="Walhaz">Walha</a>)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. The native term for the language is <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Cymraeg</i>: North/Central Wales pronunciation /k\xc9\x99m\'ra\xc9\xaag/, South Wales pronunciation /k\xc9\x99m\'ra:g/.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages#Classification" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages \xc2\xa7&#160;Classification</a></div>\n<p>Welsh evolved from Common Brittonic, the Celtic language spoken by the ancient <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">Celtic Britons</a>. Classified as <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic_languages" title="Insular Celtic languages">Insular Celtic</a>, the British language probably arrived in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> or <a href="/wiki/British_Iron_Age" title="British Iron Age">Iron Age</a> and was probably spoken throughout the island south of the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Firth of Forth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-KochBritons_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KochBritons-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a> the British language began to fragment due to increased dialect differentiation, thus evolving into Welsh and the other Brittonic languages. It is not clear when Welsh became distinct.<sup id="cite_ref-koch_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-koch-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kenneth_H._Jackson" title="Kenneth H. Jackson">Kenneth H. Jackson</a> suggested that the evolution in syllabic structure and sound pattern was complete by around 550, and labelled the period between then and about 800 "Primitive Welsh".<sup id="cite_ref-Koch1757_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch1757-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> This Primitive Welsh may have been spoken in both Wales and the <a href="/wiki/Hen_Ogledd" title="Hen Ogledd">Hen Ogledd</a> ("Old North") \xe2\x80\x93 the Brittonic-speaking areas of what is now <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">northern England</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> \xe2\x80\x93 and therefore may have been the ancestor of Cumbric as well as Welsh. Jackson, however, believed that the two varieties were already distinct by that time.<sup id="cite_ref-koch_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-koch-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The earliest Welsh poetry \xe2\x80\x93 that attributed to the <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text"><a href="/wiki/Cynfeirdd" class="mw-redirect" title="Cynfeirdd">Cynfeirdd</a></i> or "Early Poets" \xe2\x80\x93 is generally considered to date to the Primitive Welsh period. However, much of this poetry was supposedly composed in the <a href="/wiki/Hen_Ogledd" title="Hen Ogledd">Hen Ogledd</a>, raising further questions about the dating of the material and language in which it was originally composed.<sup id="cite_ref-koch_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-koch-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> This discretion stems from the fact that Cumbric was widely believed to have been the language used in Hen Ogledd. An <a href="/wiki/8th-century" class="mw-redirect" title="8th-century">8th-century</a> inscription in <a href="/wiki/Tywyn" title="Tywyn">Tywyn</a> shows the language already dropping <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflections</a> in the declension of nouns.<sup id="cite_ref-Jenkins_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Janet Davies proposed that the origins of Welsh language were much less definite; in <i>The Welsh Language: A History</i>, she proposes that Welsh may have been around even earlier than 600 AD. This is evidenced by the dropping of final syllables from Brittonic: <i>*bardos</i> "poet" became <i>bardd</i>, and <i>*abona</i> "river" became <i>afon</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Though both Davies and Jackson cite minor changes in syllable structure and sounds as evidence for the creation of Old Welsh, Davies suggests it may be more appropriate to refer to this derivative language as <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Lingua Brittanica</i> rather than characterising it as a new language altogether.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primitive_Welsh">Primitive Welsh</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Primitive Welsh">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The argued dates for the period of "Primitive Welsh" are widely debated, with some historians\' suggestions differing by hundreds of years.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Welsh">Old Welsh</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Old Welsh">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Welsh" title="Old Welsh">Old Welsh</a></div>\n<p>The next main period is <a href="/wiki/Old_Welsh" title="Old Welsh">Old Welsh</a> (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Hen Gymraeg</i>, 9th to 11th centuries); <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a> from both Wales and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> has been preserved in this form of the language. As <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">Germanic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> colonisation of Britain proceeded, the Brittonic speakers in Wales were split off from those in northern England, speaking Cumbric, and those in the southwest, speaking what would become Cornish, and so the languages diverged. Both the works of <a href="/wiki/Aneirin" title="Aneirin">Aneirin</a> (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Canu Aneirin</i>, c. 600) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Taliesin" title="Book of Taliesin">Book of Taliesin</a></i> (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Canu Taliesin</i>) were during this era.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Welsh">Middle Welsh</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Middle Welsh">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Middle_Welsh" title="Middle Welsh">Middle Welsh</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Middle_Welsh" title="Middle Welsh">Middle Welsh</a> (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Cymraeg Canol</i>) is the label attached to the Welsh of the 12th to 14th centuries, of which much more remains than for any earlier period. This is the language of nearly all surviving early manuscripts of the <i><a href="/wiki/Mabinogion" title="Mabinogion">Mabinogion</a></i>, although the tales themselves are certainly much older. It is also the language of the existing <a href="/wiki/Welsh_law" title="Welsh law">Welsh law</a> manuscripts.  Middle Welsh is reasonably intelligible to a modern-day Welsh speaker.\n</p><p>The famous cleric <a href="/wiki/Gerald_of_Wales" title="Gerald of Wales">Gerald of Wales</a> tells, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Descriptio_Cambriae" title="Descriptio Cambriae">Descriptio Cambriae</a></i>, a story of King <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II of England</a>. During one of the King\'s many raids in the 12th century, Henry asked an old man of <a href="/wiki/Pencader,_Carmarthenshire" title="Pencader, Carmarthenshire">Pencader, Carmarthenshire</a> whether the Welsh people could resist his army.  The old man replied:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It can never be destroyed through the wrath of man, unless the wrath of God shall concur.  Nor do I think that any other nation than this of Wales, nor any other language, whatever may hereafter come to pass, shall in the day of reckoning before the Supreme Judge, answer for this corner of the Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Welsh">Modern Welsh</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern Welsh">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(December 2017)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Modern Welsh is subdivided into Early Modern Welsh and Late Modern Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Early Modern Welsh ran from the 15th century through to the end of the 16th century, and the Late Modern Welsh period roughly dates from the 16th century onwards. Contemporary Welsh differs greatly from the Welsh of the 16th century, but they are similar enough for a fluent Welsh speaker to have little trouble understanding it. During the Modern Welsh period there has been a decline in the popularity of the Welsh language: the number of Welsh speakers declined to the point at which there was concern that the language would become extinct. Welsh government processes and legislation have worked to increase the proliferation of the Welsh language, e.g. through education.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Welsh_Bible">Welsh Bible</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Welsh Bible">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg/220px-1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg" width="220" height="351" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg/330px-1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg/440px-1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg 2x" data-file-width="753" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1588 Welsh Bible</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Welsh" title="Bible translations into Welsh">Bible translations into Welsh</a> helped maintain the use of Welsh in daily life. The <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> was translated by <a href="/wiki/William_Salesbury" title="William Salesbury">William Salesbury</a> in 1567, and the complete Bible by <a href="/wiki/William_Morgan_(Bible_translator)" title="William Morgan (Bible translator)">William Morgan</a> in 1588.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wales">Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/350px-Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" width="350" height="388" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/525px-Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/700px-Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png 2x" data-file-width="13342" data-file-height="14791" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The proportion of respondents in the 2011 census who said they could speak Welsh</div></div></div>\n<p>Welsh has been spoken continuously in Wales throughout recorded history, but by 1911 it had become a minority language, spoken by 43.5 per cent of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> While this decline continued over the following decades, the language did not die out. By the start of the 21st century, numbers began to increase once more, at least partly as a result of the increase in <a href="/wiki/Welsh_medium_education" title="Welsh medium education">Welsh medium education</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The 2004 Welsh Language Use Survey showed that 21.7 per cent of the population of Wales spoke Welsh,<sup id="cite_ref-2004Survey_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2004Survey-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> compared with 20.8 per cent in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2001">2001 Census</a>, and 18.5 per cent in 1991 Census. The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011 Census</a>, however, showed a slight decline to 562,000, or 19 per cent of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\nThe census also showed a "big drop" in the number of speakers in the Welsh-speaking heartlands, with the number dropping to under 50 per cent in <a href="/wiki/Ceredigion" title="Ceredigion">Ceredigion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carmarthenshire" title="Carmarthenshire">Carmarthenshire</a> for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> However, according to the Welsh Language Use Survey in 2013\xe2\x80\x9315, 24 per cent of people aged three and over were able to speak Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In 2018 a survey by the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics" title="Office for National Statistics">ONS</a> suggested 29.3 per cent of the population of Wales were able to speak the language.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historically, large numbers of Welsh people spoke only Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Over the course of the 20th century this monolingual population "all but disappeared", but a small percentage remained at the time of the 1981 census.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Most Welsh-speaking people in Wales also speak English (while in <a href="/wiki/Chubut_Province" title="Chubut Province">Chubut Province</a>, Argentina, most speakers can speak Spanish \xe2\x80\x93 see <a href="/wiki/Y_Wladfa" title="Y Wladfa">Y Wladfa</a>). However, many Welsh-speaking people are more comfortable expressing themselves in Welsh than in English. A speaker\'s choice of language can vary according to the subject domain and the social context, even within a single <a href="/wiki/Discourse" title="Discourse">discourse</a> (known in linguistics as <a href="/wiki/Code-switching" title="Code-switching">code-switching</a>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Welsh speakers are largely concentrated in the north and west of Wales, principally <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd" title="Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conwy_(county_borough)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conwy (county borough)">Conwy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denbighshire" title="Denbighshire">Denbighshire</a> (<i>Sir Ddinbych</i>), <a href="/wiki/Anglesey" title="Anglesey">Anglesey</a> (<i>Ynys M\xc3\xb4n</i>), Carmarthenshire (<i>Sir G\xc3\xa2r</i>), north <a href="/wiki/Pembrokeshire" title="Pembrokeshire">Pembrokeshire</a> (<i>Sir Benfro</i>), Ceredigion, parts of <a href="/wiki/Glamorgan" title="Glamorgan">Glamorgan</a> (<i>Morgannwg</i>), and north-west and extreme south-west <a href="/wiki/Powys" title="Powys">Powys</a>. However, first-language and other fluent speakers can be found throughout Wales.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outside_Wales">Outside Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Outside Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Welsh-speaking communities persisted well into the modern period across the border in England. <a href="/wiki/Archenfield" title="Archenfield">Archenfield</a> was still Welsh enough in the time of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Hereford" title="Bishop of Hereford">Bishop of Hereford</a> to be made responsible, together with the four Welsh bishops, for the translation of the Bible and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a> into Welsh. Welsh was still commonly spoken here in the first half of the 19th century, and churchwardens\' notices were put up in both Welsh and English until about 1860.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In one of the earliest works of <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">phonetics</a>, <i>On Early English Pronunciation</i> in 1889, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_John_Ellis" title="Alexander John Ellis">Alexander John Ellis</a> identified a small part of <a href="/wiki/Shropshire" title="Shropshire">Shropshire</a> as still speaking Welsh, and plotted a Celtic border that passed from <a href="/wiki/Llanymynech" title="Llanymynech">Llanymynech</a> to <a href="/wiki/Chirk" title="Chirk">Chirk</a> through <a href="/wiki/Oswestry" title="Oswestry">Oswestry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The number of Welsh-speaking people in the rest of Britain has not yet been counted for statistical purposes. In 1993, the Welsh-language television channel <a href="/wiki/S4C" title="S4C">S4C</a> published the results of a survey into the numbers of people who spoke or understood Welsh, which estimated that there were around 133,000 Welsh-speaking people living in England, about 50,000 of them in the Greater London area.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Language_Board" title="Welsh Language Board">Welsh Language Board</a>, on the basis of an analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics" title="Office for National Statistics">Office for National Statistics</a> (ONS) Longitudinal Study, estimated there were 110,000 Welsh-speaking people in England, and another thousand in Scotland and Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011 Census</a>, 8,248 people in England gave Welsh in answer to the question "What is your main language?"<sup id="cite_ref-nomisweb1_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nomisweb1-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The ONS subsequently published a census glossary of terms to support the release of results from the census, including their definition of "main language" as referring to "first or preferred language" (though that wording was not in the census questionnaire itself).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The wards in England with the most people giving Welsh as their main language were the <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> wards of <a href="/wiki/Central_(Liverpool_ward)" title="Central (Liverpool ward)">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenbank_(ward)" title="Greenbank (ward)">Greenbank</a>, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Oswestry_South&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Oswestry South (page does not exist)">Oswestry South</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nomisweb1_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nomisweb1-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> In terms of the <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_England" title="Regions of England">regions of England</a>, North West England (1,945), London (1,310) and the West Midlands (1,265) had the highest number of people noting Welsh as their main language.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The American Community Survey 2009\xe2\x80\x932013 noted that 2,235 people aged 5 years and over in the United States spoke Welsh at home. The highest number of those (255) lived in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><span id="Current_status"></span>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">Official status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Official status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Estaci%C3%B3n_Gaiman_(2).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Estaci%C3%B3n_Gaiman_%282%29.JPG/220px-Estaci%C3%B3n_Gaiman_%282%29.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Estaci%C3%B3n_Gaiman_%282%29.JPG/330px-Estaci%C3%B3n_Gaiman_%282%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Estaci%C3%B3n_Gaiman_%282%29.JPG/440px-Estaci%C3%B3n_Gaiman_%282%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Estaci%C3%B3n_Gaiman_(2).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trilingual (Spanish, Welsh and English) sign in Argentina</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg/220px-Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg" width="220" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg/330px-Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg/440px-Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="670" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Road_signs_in_Wales" title="Road signs in Wales">Bilingual road markings</a> near <a href="/wiki/Cardiff_Airport" title="Cardiff Airport">Cardiff Airport</a>. In Welsh-speaking areas, the Welsh signage appears first.</div></div></div>\n<p>Although Welsh is a minority language, support for it grew during the second half of the 20th century, along with the rise of organisations such as the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_nationalism" title="Welsh nationalism">nationalist</a> political party <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text"><a href="/wiki/Plaid_Cymru" title="Plaid Cymru">Plaid Cymru</a></i> from 1925 and <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Language_Society" title="Welsh Language Society">Welsh Language Society</a> from 1962.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Language_Act_1993" title="Welsh Language Act 1993">Welsh Language Act 1993</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Wales_Act_1998" title="Government of Wales Act 1998">Government of Wales Act 1998</a> provide that the Welsh and English languages be treated equally in the public sector, as far as is reasonable and practicable. Each public body is required to prepare for approval a <a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_Language_Scheme&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Welsh Language Scheme (page does not exist)">Welsh Language Scheme</a>, which indicates its commitment to the equality of treatment principle. This is sent out in draft form for public consultation for a three-month period, whereupon comments on it may be incorporated into a final version. It requires the final approval of the now defunct <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Language_Board" title="Welsh Language Board">Welsh Language Board</a> (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Bwrdd yr Iaith Gymraeg</i>). Thereafter, the public body is charged with implementing and fulfilling its obligations under the Welsh Language Scheme. The list of other public bodies which have to prepare Schemes could be added to by initially the Secretary of State for Wales, from 1993\xe2\x80\x931997, by way of <a href="/wiki/Statutory_Instrument_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Statutory Instrument (UK)">statutory instrument</a>. Subsequent to the forming of the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">National Assembly for Wales</a> in 1997, the Government Minister responsible for the Welsh language can and has passed statutory instruments naming public bodies who have to prepare Schemes. Neither the 1993 Act nor secondary legislation made under it covers the private sector, although some organisations, notably banks and some railway companies, provide some of their information in Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 7 December 2010, the Welsh Assembly unanimously approved a set of measures to develop the use of the Welsh language within Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbcnews2010_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcnews2010-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> On 9 February 2011 this measure, the Proposed Welsh Language (Wales) Measure 2011, was passed and received Royal Assent, thus making the Welsh language an officially recognised language within Wales. The Measure:\n</p>\n<ul><li>confirms the official status of the Welsh language;</li>\n<li>creates a new system of placing duties on bodies to provide services through the medium of Welsh;</li>\n<li>creates a Welsh Language Commissioner with strong enforcement powers to protect the rights of Welsh-speaking people to access services through the medium of Welsh;</li>\n<li>establishes a Welsh Language Tribunal;</li>\n<li>gives individuals and bodies the right to appeal decisions made in relation to the provision of services through the medium of Welsh</li>\n<li>creates a Welsh Language Partnership Council to advise Government on its strategy in relation to the Welsh language;</li>\n<li>allows for an official investigation by the Welsh Language Commissioner of instances where there is an attempt to interfere with the freedom of Welsh-speaking people to use the language with one another.<sup id="cite_ref-wales.gov.uk_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wales.gov.uk-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>With the passing of this measure, public bodies and some private companies are required to provide services in Welsh. The Welsh government\'s Minister for Heritage at the time, <a href="/wiki/Alun_Ffred_Jones" title="Alun Ffred Jones">Alun Ffred Jones</a>, said, "The Welsh language is a source of great pride for the people of Wales, whether they speak it or not, and I am delighted that this Measure has now become law. I am very proud to have steered legislation through the Assembly which confirms the official status of the Welsh language; which creates a strong advocate for Welsh speakers and will improve the quality and quantity of services available through the medium of Welsh. I believe that everyone who wants to access services in the Welsh language should be able to do so, and that is what this government has worked towards. This legislation is an important and historic step forward for the language, its speakers and for the nation."<sup id="cite_ref-wales.gov.uk_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wales.gov.uk-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The measure was not welcomed warmly by all supporters: Bethan Williams, chairperson of the Welsh Language Society, gave a mixed response to the move, saying, "Through this measure we have won official status for the language and that has been warmly welcomed. But there was a core principle missing in the law passed by the Assembly before Christmas. It doesn\'t give language rights to the people of Wales in every aspect of their lives. Despite that, an amendment to that effect was supported by 18 Assembly Members from three different parties, and that was a significant step forward."<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 5 October 2011, Meri Huws, Chair of the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Language_Board" title="Welsh Language Board">Welsh Language Board</a>, was appointed the new Welsh Language Commissioner.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> She released a statement that she was "delighted" to have been appointed to the "hugely important role", adding, "I look forward to working with the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Government" title="Welsh Government">Welsh Government</a> and organisations in Wales in developing the new system of standards. I will look to build on the good work that has been done by the Welsh Language Board and others to strengthen the Welsh language and ensure that it continues to thrive." First Minister <a href="/wiki/Carwyn_Jones" title="Carwyn Jones">Carwyn Jones</a> said that Meri would act as a champion for the Welsh language, though some had concerns over her appointment: <a href="/wiki/Plaid_Cymru" title="Plaid Cymru">Plaid Cymru</a> spokeswoman Bethan Jenkins said, "I have concerns about the transition from Meri Huws\'s role from the Welsh Language Board to the language commissioner, and I will be asking the Welsh government how this will be successfully managed. We must be sure that there is no conflict of interest, and that the Welsh Language Commissioner can demonstrate how she will offer the required fresh approach to this new role." Ms Huws started her role as the Welsh Language Commissioner on 1 April 2012.\n</p><p>Local councils and the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">National Assembly for Wales</a> use Welsh, issuing Welsh versions of their literature, to varying degrees.\n</p><p>Most road signs in Wales are in English and Welsh.\n</p><p>Since 2000, the teaching of Welsh has been compulsory in all schools in Wales up to age 16. That has had an effect in stabilising and reversing the decline in the language. It means, for example, that even the children of non-Welsh-speaking parents from elsewhere in the UK grow up with a knowledge of, or complete fluency in, the language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The wording on currency is only in English, except in the legend on Welsh pound coins dated 1985, 1990 and 1995, which circulate in all parts of the UK. The wording is <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Pleidiol wyf i\'m gwlad</i>, (Welsh&#160;for True am I to my country), and derives from the national anthem of Wales, <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text"><a href="/wiki/Hen_Wlad_Fy_Nhadau" title="Hen Wlad Fy Nhadau">Hen Wlad Fy Nhadau</a></i>.\n</p><p>Some shops employ bilingual signage. Welsh rarely appears on product packaging or instructions.\n</p><p>The UK government has ratified the <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a> in respect of Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Welsh_singe_in_Wrexham_1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Welsh_singe_in_Wrexham_1.png/220px-Welsh_singe_in_Wrexham_1.png" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Welsh_singe_in_Wrexham_1.png/330px-Welsh_singe_in_Wrexham_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Welsh_singe_in_Wrexham_1.png/440px-Welsh_singe_in_Wrexham_1.png 2x" data-file-width="1718" data-file-height="1174" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Welsh_singe_in_Wrexham_1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bilingual road sign near <a href="/wiki/Wrexham_Central_railway_station" title="Wrexham Central railway station">Wrexham Central station</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The language has greatly increased its prominence since the creation of the television channel <a href="/wiki/S4C" title="S4C">S4C</a> in November 1982, which until <a href="/wiki/Digital_television_transition" title="Digital television transition">digital switchover</a> in 2010 broadcast 70 per cent of Channel 4\'s programming along with a majority of Welsh language shows<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> during peak viewing hours. The all-Welsh-language digital station S4C Digidol is available throughout Europe on satellite and online throughout the UK. Since the digital switchover was completed in South Wales on 31 March 2010, S4C Digidol became the main broadcasting channel and fully in Welsh. The main evening television news provided by the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> in Welsh is available for download.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> There is also a Welsh-language radio station, <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_Cymru" title="BBC Radio Cymru">BBC Radio Cymru</a>, which was launched in 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The only Welsh-language national newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Y_Cymro" title="Y Cymro">Y Cymro</a></i> (<i>The Welshman</i>) was published weekly until 2017. There is no daily newspaper in Welsh. A daily newspaper called <i><a href="/wiki/Y_Byd" title="Y Byd">Y Byd</a></i> (<i>The World</i>) was scheduled to be launched on 3 March 2008, but was scrapped,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> owing to poor sales of subscriptions<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Government" title="Welsh Government">Welsh Government</a> deeming the publication not to meet the criteria necessary for the kind of public funding it needed to be rescued.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> There is a Welsh-language online news service which publishes news stories in Welsh called <a href="/wiki/Golwg360" title="Golwg360">Golwg360</a> ("360 [degree] view").\n</p><p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_education">In education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: In education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_medium_education" title="Welsh medium education">Welsh medium education</a></div>\n<p>The decade around 1840 was a period of great social upheaval in Wales, manifested in the <a href="/wiki/Chartism" title="Chartism">Chartist</a> movement. In 1839, 20,000 people marched on <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Wales" title="Newport, Wales">Newport</a>, resulting in a riot when 20 people were killed by soldiers defending the Westgate Hotel, and the <a href="/wiki/Rebecca_Riots" title="Rebecca Riots">Rebecca Riots</a> where <a href="/wiki/Toll_road" title="Toll road">tollbooths</a> on <a href="/wiki/Turnpikes" class="mw-redirect" title="Turnpikes">turnpikes</a> were systematically destroyed.\n</p><p>This unrest brought the state of education in Wales to the attention of the English establishment since social reformers of the time considered education as a means of dealing with social ills. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> newspaper was prominent among those who considered that the lack of education of the Welsh people was the root cause of most of the problems.\n</p><p>In July 1846, three commissioners, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Lingen,_1st_Baron_Lingen" title="Ralph Lingen, 1st Baron Lingen">R.R.W. Lingen</a>, Jellynger C. Symons and H.R. Vaughan Johnson, were appointed to inquire into the state of education in Wales; the Commissioners were all <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicans</a> and thus presumed unsympathetic to the <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">nonconformist</a> majority in Wales. The Commissioners presented their report to the Government on 1 July 1847 in three large blue-bound volumes. This report quickly became known as the <a href="/wiki/Treachery_of_the_Blue_Books" title="Treachery of the Blue Books">Treachery of the Blue Books</a> (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Brad y Llyfrau Gleision</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> since, apart from documenting the state of education in Wales, the Commissioners were also free with their comments disparaging the language, <a href="/wiki/Nonconformity_in_Wales" title="Nonconformity in Wales">nonconformity</a>, and the morals of the Welsh people in general. An immediate effect of the report was that ordinary Welsh people began to believe that the only way to get on in the world was through the medium of English, and an inferiority complex developed about the Welsh language whose effects have not yet been completely eradicated. The historian Professor <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_O._Morgan" title="Kenneth O. Morgan">Kenneth O. Morgan</a> referred to the significance of the report and its consequences as "the <a href="/wiki/Massacre_of_Glencoe" title="Massacre of Glencoe">Glencoe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jallianwala_Bagh_massacre" title="Jallianwala Bagh massacre">Amritsar</a> of Welsh history".<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Welshclass.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Welshclass.jpg/220px-Welshclass.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Welshclass.jpg/330px-Welshclass.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Welshclass.jpg/440px-Welshclass.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Welshclass.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Welsh language as the medium of instruction</div></div></div>\n<p>In the later 19th century, virtually all teaching in the schools of Wales was in English, even in areas where the pupils barely understood English. Some schools used the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Not" title="Welsh Not">Welsh Not</a>, a piece of wood, often bearing the letters "WN", which was hung around the neck of any pupil caught speaking Welsh. The pupil could pass it on to any schoolmate heard speaking Welsh, with the pupil wearing it at the end of the day being given a beating. One of the most famous Welsh-born pioneers of higher education in Wales was <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Owen_(educator)" title="Hugh Owen (educator)">Sir Hugh Owen</a>. He made great progress in the cause of education, and more especially the <a href="/wiki/Aberystwyth_University" title="Aberystwyth University">University College of Wales</a> at <a href="/wiki/Aberystwyth" title="Aberystwyth">Aberystwyth</a>, of which he was chief founder. He has been credited<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2009)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> with the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_Intermediate_Education_Act_1889&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Welsh Intermediate Education Act 1889 (page does not exist)">Welsh Intermediate Education Act 1889</a> (52 &amp; 53 Vict c 40), following which several new Welsh schools were built. The first was completed in 1894 and named <a href="/wiki/Ysgol_Syr_Hugh_Owen" title="Ysgol Syr Hugh Owen">Ysgol Syr Hugh Owen</a>.\n</p><p>Towards the beginning of the 20th century this policy slowly began to change, partly owing to the efforts of <a href="/wiki/Owen_Morgan_Edwards" title="Owen Morgan Edwards">O.M. Edwards</a> when he became chief inspector of schools for Wales in 1907.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ysgol_Gymraeg_Aberystwyth" title="Ysgol Gymraeg Aberystwyth">Aberystwyth Welsh School</a> (<span lang="cy" style="font-style:normal;" title="Welsh language text">Ysgol Gymraeg Aberystwyth</span>) was founded in 1939 by <a href="/wiki/Ifan_ab_Owen_Edwards" title="Ifan ab Owen Edwards">Sir Ifan ap Owen Edwards</a>, the son of O.M. Edwards, as the first Welsh Primary School.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The headteacher was <a href="/wiki/Norah_Isaac" title="Norah Isaac">Norah Isaac</a>. <span lang="cy" style="font-style:normal;" title="Welsh language text">Ysgol Gymraeg Aberystwyth</span> is still a very successful school, and now there are Welsh-language primary schools all over the country. <span lang="cy" style="font-style:normal;" title="Welsh language text"><a href="/wiki/Ysgol_Glan_Clwyd" title="Ysgol Glan Clwyd">Ysgol Glan Clwyd</a></span> was established in <a href="/wiki/Rhyl" title="Rhyl">Rhyl</a> in 1956 as the first Welsh-medium secondary school.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Defnyddiwch_eich_Cymraeg_-_Use_your_Welsh_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488577.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Defnyddiwch_eich_Cymraeg_-_Use_your_Welsh_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488577.jpg/220px-Defnyddiwch_eich_Cymraeg_-_Use_your_Welsh_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488577.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Defnyddiwch_eich_Cymraeg_-_Use_your_Welsh_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488577.jpg/330px-Defnyddiwch_eich_Cymraeg_-_Use_your_Welsh_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488577.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Defnyddiwch_eich_Cymraeg_-_Use_your_Welsh_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488577.jpg/440px-Defnyddiwch_eich_Cymraeg_-_Use_your_Welsh_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488577.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="428" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Defnyddiwch_eich_Cymraeg_-_Use_your_Welsh_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488577.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sign promoting the learning of Welsh</div></div></div>\n<p>Welsh is now widely used in education, with 101,345 children and young people in Wales receiving their education in Welsh medium schools in 2014/15, 65,460 in primary and 35,885 in secondary.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> 26 per cent of all schools in Wales are defined as Welsh medium schools, with a further 7.3 per cent offering some Welsh-medium instruction to pupils.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> 22 per cent of pupils are in schools in which Welsh the primary language of instruction.  Under the <a href="/wiki/National_Curriculum_for_Wales" title="National Curriculum for Wales">National Curriculum</a>, it is compulsory that all students study Welsh up to the age of 16 as either a first or a second language.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Some students choose to continue with their studies through the medium of Welsh for the completion of their A-levels as well as during their college years. All <a href="/wiki/Local_education_authority" title="Local education authority">local education authorities</a> in Wales have schools providing bilingual or Welsh-<a href="/wiki/Medium_of_instruction" title="Medium of instruction">medium</a> education.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The remainder study Welsh as a second language in English-medium schools. Specialist teachers of Welsh called <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Athrawon Bro</i> support the teaching of Welsh in the National Curriculum. Welsh is also taught in adult education classes. The Welsh Government has recently set up six centres of excellence in the teaching of Welsh for Adults, with centres in North Wales,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Mid Wales, South West, Glamorgan, Gwent. and Cardiff.\n</p><p>The ability to speak Welsh or to have Welsh as a qualification is desirable for certain career choices in Wales, such as teaching or customer service.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> All universities in Wales teach courses in the language, with many undergraduate and post-graduate degree programs offered in the medium of Welsh, ranging from law, modern languages, social sciences, and also other sciences such as biological sciences. Aberystwyth, Cardiff, Bangor, and Swansea have all had chairs in Welsh since their virtual establishment, and all their schools of Welsh are successful centres for the study of the Welsh language and its literature, offering a BA in Welsh as well as post-graduate courses. At all Welsh universities and the <a href="/wiki/Open_University" title="Open University">Open University</a>, students have the right to submit assessed work and sit exams in Welsh even if the course was taught in English (usually the only exception is where the course requires demonstrating proficiency in another language). Following a commitment made in the One Wales coalition government between Labour and Plaid Cymru, the <a href="/wiki/Coleg_Cymraeg_Cenedlaethol" title="Coleg Cymraeg Cenedlaethol">Coleg Cymraeg Cenedlaethol</a> (Welsh Language National College) was established. The purpose of the federal structured college, spread out between all the universities of Wales, is to provide and also advance Welsh medium courses and Welsh medium scholarship and research in Welsh universities. There is also a Welsh-medium academic journal called <i>Gwerddon</i> ("Oasis"), which is a platform for academic research in Welsh and is published quarterly. There have been calls for more teaching of Welsh in English-medium schools.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_information_technology">In information technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: In information technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Celtic-language_media" title="List of Celtic-language media">List of Celtic-language media</a></div>\n<p>Like many of the world\'s languages, the Welsh language has seen an increased use and presence on the internet, ranging from formal lists of terminology in a variety of fields<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> to Welsh language interfaces for <a href="/wiki/Windows_7" title="Windows 7">Windows 7</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows_XP" class="mw-redirect" title="Microsoft Windows XP">Microsoft Windows XP</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windows_Vista" title="Windows Vista">Vista</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office" title="Microsoft Office">Microsoft Office</a>, <a href="/wiki/LibreOffice" title="LibreOffice">LibreOffice</a>, <a href="/wiki/OpenOffice.org" title="OpenOffice.org">OpenOffice.org</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozilla_Firefox" class="mw-redirect" title="Mozilla Firefox">Mozilla Firefox</a> and a variety of <a href="/wiki/Linux_distribution" title="Linux distribution">Linux distributions</a>, and on-line services to <a href="/wiki/Blog" title="Blog">blogs</a> kept in Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Wikipedia has had a Welsh version since July 2003 and <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a> since 2009.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mobile_phone_technology">Mobile phone technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mobile phone technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 2006 the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Language_Board" title="Welsh Language Board">Welsh Language Board</a> launched a free software pack which enabled the use of <a href="/wiki/SMS" title="SMS">SMS</a> <a href="/wiki/Predictive_text" title="Predictive text">predictive text</a> in Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>  At the <a href="/wiki/National_Eisteddfod_of_Wales" title="National Eisteddfod of Wales">National Eisteddfod of Wales</a> 2009, a further announcement was made by the Welsh Language Board that the mobile phone company <a href="/wiki/Samsung" title="Samsung">Samsung</a> was to work with the network provider <a href="/wiki/Orange_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Orange (UK)">Orange</a> to provide the first mobile phone in the Welsh language,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> with the interface and the T9 dictionary on the Samsung S5600 available in the Welsh language. The model, available with the Welsh language interface, has been available since 1 September 2009, with plans to introduce it on other networks.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> devices, both the built-in <a href="/wiki/Google_Keyboard" class="mw-redirect" title="Google Keyboard">Google Keyboard</a> and user-created keyboards can be used.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a> devices have fully supported the Welsh language since the release of iOS 8 in September 2014. Users can switch their device to Welsh to access apps that are available in Welsh. Date and time on iOS is also localised, as shown by the built-in Calendar application, as well as certain third party apps that have been localized.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_warfare">In warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: In warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Secure communications are often difficult to achieve in wartime.  <a href="/wiki/Cryptography" title="Cryptography">Cryptography</a> can be used to protect messages, but codes can be broken. Therefore, lesser-known languages are sometimes encoded, so that even if the code is broken, the message is still in a language few people know. For example, <a href="/wiki/Navajo_language" title="Navajo language">Navajo</a> <a href="/wiki/Code_talker" title="Code talker">code talkers</a> were used by the <a href="/wiki/Military_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Military of the United States">United States military</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Welch_Fusiliers" title="Royal Welch Fusiliers">Royal Welch Fusiliers</a>, a Welsh regiment serving in <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia</a>, used Welsh for emergency communications that needed to be secure.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>  It has been reported that Welsh speakers from Wales and from <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a> fought on both sides in the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_within_the_British_parliament">Use within the British parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Use within the British parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 2017, parliamentary rules were amended to allow the use of Welsh when the Welsh Grand Committee meets at <a href="/wiki/British_parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="British parliament">Westminster</a>. The change did not alter the rules about debates within the House of Commons, where only English can be used.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In February 2018, Welsh was first used when the Welsh Secretary, <a href="/wiki/Alun_Cairns" title="Alun Cairns">Alun Cairns</a>, delivered his welcoming speech at a sitting of the committee. He said, "I am proud to be using the language I grew up speaking, which is not only important to me, my family and the communities Welsh MPs represent, but is also an integral part of Welsh history and culture".<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_at_the_European_Union">Use at the European Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Use at the European Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In November 2008, the Welsh language was used at a meeting of the European Union\'s <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Council of the European Union">Council of Ministers</a> for the first time. The Heritage Minister <a href="/wiki/Alun_Ffred_Jones" title="Alun Ffred Jones">Alun Ffred Jones</a> addressed his audience in Welsh and his words were interpreted into the EU\'s 23 official languages. The official use of the language followed years of campaigning.  Jones said "In the UK we have one of the world\'s major languages, English, as the mother tongue of many. But there is a diversity of languages within our islands. I am proud to be speaking to you in one of the oldest of these, Welsh, the language of Wales." He described the breakthrough as "more than [merely] symbolic" saying "Welsh might be one of the oldest languages to be used in the UK, but it remains one of the most vibrant. Our literature, our arts, our festivals, our great tradition of song all find expression through our language. And this is a powerful demonstration of how our culture, the very essence of who we are, is expressed through language."<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_by_the_Voyager_program">Use by the Voyager program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Use by the Voyager program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A greeting in Welsh is one of the <a href="/wiki/Contents_of_the_Voyager_Golden_Record#Greetings" title="Contents of the Voyager Golden Record">55 languages</a> included on the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_Golden_Record" title="Voyager Golden Record">Voyager Golden Record</a> chosen to be representative of Earth in NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager program</a> launched in 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The greetings are unique to each language, with the Welsh greeting being <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Iechyd da i chwi yn awr ac yn oesoedd</i>, which translates into English as "Good health to you now and forever".<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Welsh vocabulary draws mainly from original Brittonic words (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">wy</i> "egg", <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">carreg</i> "stone"), with some loans <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Category:cy:Latin_derivations" class="extiw" title="wikt:Category:cy:Latin derivations">from Latin</a> (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">ffenestr</i> "window" &lt; Latin <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">fenestra</i>, <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">gwin</i> "wine" &lt; Latin <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">vinum</i>), and <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">silff</i> "shelf", <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">gi\xc3\xa2t</i> "gate").\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_phonology" title="Welsh phonology">Welsh phonology</a></div>\n<table role="presentation" class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA"><img alt="[]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/30px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png" width="30" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/45px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/60px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="75" data-file-height="100" /></a></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> phonetic symbols.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA#Rendering_issues" title="Help:IPA">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>&#32;instead of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a> of Welsh includes a number of sounds that do not occur in English and are <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_typology" title="Linguistic typology">typologically</a> rare in <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Europe" title="Languages of Europe">European languages</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_and_alveolar_lateral_fricatives" title="Voiceless dental and alveolar lateral fricatives">voiceless alveolar lateral fricative</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xac]</span>, the <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_nasal" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless nasal">voiceless nasals</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[m\xcc\xa5]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[n\xcc\xa5]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc5\x8b\xcc\x8a]</span>, and the <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_trill" title="Voiceless alveolar trill">voiceless alveolar trill</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[r\xcc\xa5]</span> are distinctive features of the Welsh language. <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">Stress</a> usually falls on the penultimate syllable in polysyllabic words, and the word-final unstressed syllable receives a higher <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(music)" title="Pitch (music)">pitch</a> than the stressed syllable.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Orthography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_orthography" title="Welsh orthography">Welsh orthography</a></div>\n<p>Welsh is written in a <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin alphabet</a> of 29 letters, of which eight are <a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">digraphs</a> treated as separate letters for <a href="/wiki/Collation" title="Collation">collation</a>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>a, b, c, ch, d, dd, e, f, ff, g, ng, h, i, j, l, ll, m, n, o, p, ph, r, rh, s, t, th, u, w, y</dd></dl>\n<p>In contrast to English practice, "w" and "y" are considered vowel letters in Welsh along with "a", "e", "i", "o" and "u".\n</p><p>The letter "j" was not used traditionally, but is now used in many everyday words borrowed from English, like <i>jam</i>, <i>j\xc3\xb4c</i> "joke" and <i>garej</i> "garage". The letters "k", "q", "v", "x", and "z" are used in some technical terms, like <i>kilogram</i>, <i>volt</i> and <i>zero</i>, but in all cases can be, and often are, replaced by Welsh letters: <i>cilogram</i>, <i>folt</i> and <i>sero.</i><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The letter "k" was in common use until the 16th century, but was dropped at the time of the publication of the New Testament in Welsh, as <a href="/wiki/William_Salesbury" title="William Salesbury">William Salesbury</a> explained: "C for K, because the printers have not so many as the Welsh requireth".
