This change was not popular at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most common <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">diacritic</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Circumflex" title="Circumflex">circumflex</a>, which disambiguates <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">long vowels</a>, most often in the case of homographs, where the vowel is short in one word and long in the other: e.g. <i>man</i> "place" vs <i>m\xc3\xa2n</i> "fine", "small".\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Colloquial_Welsh_morphology" title="Colloquial Welsh morphology">Colloquial Welsh morphology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Literary_Welsh_morphology" title="Literary Welsh morphology">Literary Welsh morphology</a></div>\n<p><span id="mutation"></span><span id="mutations"></span>\nWelsh <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a> has much in common with that of the other modern <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic_languages" title="Insular Celtic languages">Insular Celtic languages</a>, such as the use of initial <a href="/wiki/Consonant_mutation" title="Consonant mutation">consonant mutations</a> and of so-called "<a href="/wiki/Inflected_preposition" title="Inflected preposition">conjugated prepositions</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Preposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Preposition">prepositions</a> that fuse with the <a href="/wiki/Personal_pronoun" title="Personal pronoun">personal pronouns</a> that are their <a href="/wiki/Object_(grammar)" title="Object (grammar)">object</a>). Welsh nouns belong to one of two <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">grammatical genders</a>, masculine and feminine, but they are not inflected for <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">case</a>. Welsh has a variety of different endings and other methods to indicate the plural, and two endings to indicate the <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">singular</a> of some nouns. In spoken Welsh, verbal features are indicated primarily by the use of <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">auxiliary verbs</a> rather than by the <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">inflection of the main verb</a>. In literary Welsh, on the other hand, inflection of the main verb is usual.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax">Syntax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Syntax">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_syntax" title="Welsh syntax">Welsh syntax</a></div>\n<p>The canonical word order in Welsh is <a href="/wiki/Verb%E2%80%93subject%E2%80%93object" title="Verb\xe2\x80\x93subject\xe2\x80\x93object">verb\xe2\x80\x93subject\xe2\x80\x93object</a> (VSO).\n</p><p>Colloquial Welsh inclines very strongly towards the use of auxiliaries with its verbs, as in English. The present tense is constructed with <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">bod</i> ("to be") as an <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">auxiliary verb</a>, with the main verb appearing as a <a href="/wiki/Verbnoun" title="Verbnoun">verbnoun</a> (used in a way loosely equivalent to an infinitive) after the particle <i>yn</i>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Mae Si\xc3\xa2n yn mynd i Lanelli</i></dd>\n<dd>Si\xc3\xa2n is going to Llanelli.</dd></dl>\n<p>There, <i>mae</i> is a third-person singular present indicative form of <i>bod</i>, and <i>mynd</i> is the verbnoun meaning "to go". The <a href="/wiki/Imperfect" title="Imperfect">imperfect</a> is constructed in a similar manner, as are the <a href="/wiki/Periphrastic" class="mw-redirect" title="Periphrastic">periphrastic</a> forms of the <a href="/wiki/Future_tense" title="Future tense">future</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conditional_mood" title="Conditional mood">conditional</a> tenses.\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Preterite" title="Preterite">preterite</a>, future and conditional mood tenses, there are <a href="/wiki/Inflected_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Inflected language">inflected</a> forms of all verbs, which are used in the written language. However, speech now more commonly uses the verbnoun together with an inflected form of <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">gwneud</i> ("do"), so "I went" can be <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Mi es i</i> or <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Mi wnes i fynd</i> ("I did go"). <i>Mi</i> is an example of a preverbal particle; such particles are common in Welsh.\n</p><p>Welsh lacks separate pronouns for constructing subordinate clauses; instead, special verb forms or relative pronouns that appear identical to some preverbal particles are used.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Possessives_as_direct_objects_of_verbnouns">Possessives as direct objects of verbnouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Possessives as direct objects of verbnouns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Welsh for "I like Rhodri" is <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Dw i\'n hoffi Rhodri</i> (word for word, "am I in [the] liking [of] Rhodri"), with <i>Rhodri</i> in a possessive relationship with <i>hoffi</i>. With personal pronouns, the possessive form of the personal pronoun is used, as in "I like <i>him</i>": [Dw i\'n <i>ei</i> hoffi], literally, "am I <i>his</i> liking" \xe2\x80\x93 "I like <i>you</i>" is [Dw i\'n <i>dy</i> hoffi] ("am I <i>your</i> liking").\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronoun_doubling">Pronoun doubling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Pronoun doubling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In colloquial Welsh, possessive pronouns, whether they are used to mean "my", "your", etc. or to indicate the direct object of a verbnoun, are commonly reinforced by the use of the corresponding personal pronoun after the noun or verbnoun: <span lang="cy" title="Welsh language text"><i>ei d\xc5\xb7</i> e</span> "his house" (literally "his house <i>of him</i>"), <span lang="cy" title="Welsh language text"><i>Dw i\'n dy hoffi</i> di</span> "I like you" ("I am [engaged in the action of] your liking <i>of you</i>"), etc. It should be noted that the "reinforcement" (or, simply, "redoubling") adds no emphasis in the colloquial register. While the possessive pronoun alone may be used, especially in more formal registers, as shown above, it is considered incorrect to use only the personal pronoun. Such usage is nevertheless sometimes heard in very colloquial speech, mainly among young speakers: <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Ble \'dyn ni\'n mynd? T\xc5\xb7 ti neu d\xc5\xb7 fi?</i> ("Where are we going? Your house or my house?").\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Counting_system">Counting system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Counting system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_numerals" title="Welsh numerals">Welsh numerals</a></div>\n<p>The traditional counting system used in the Welsh language is <a href="/wiki/Vigesimal" title="Vigesimal">vigesimal</a>, i.e. it is based on twenties, as in standard French numbers 70 (<i lang="fr" title="French language text">soixante-dix</i>, literally "sixty-ten") to 99 (<i lang="fr" title="French language text">quatre-vingt-dix-neuf</i>, literally "four score nineteen"). Welsh numbers from 11 to 14 are "<i>x</i> on ten" (e.g. <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">un ar ddeg</i>: 11), 16 to 19 are "<i>x</i> on fifteen" (e.g. <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">un ar bymtheg</i>: 16), though 18 is <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">deunaw</i>, "two nines"; numbers from 21 to 39 are "1\xe2\x80\x9319 on twenty"(e.g. <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">deg ar hugain</i>: 30), 40 is <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">deugain</i> "two twenties", 60 is <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">trigain</i> "three twenties", etc. This form continues to be used, especially by older people, and it is obligatory in certain circumstances (such as telling the time, and in ordinal numbers).<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is also a decimal counting system, which has become relatively widely used, though less so in giving the time, ages, and dates (it features no ordinal numbers). This system is in especially common use in schools due to its simplicity, and in Patagonian Welsh. Whereas 39 in the vigesimal system is <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">pedwar ar bymtheg ar hugain</i> ("four on fifteen on twenty") or even <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">deugain namyn un</i> ("two score minus one"), in the decimal system it is <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">tri deg naw</i> ("three tens nine").\n</p><p>Although there is only one word for "one" (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">un</i>), it triggers the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_morphology" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh morphology">soft mutation</a> (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">treiglad meddal</i>) of feminine nouns, where possible, other than those beginning with "ll" or "rh". There are separate masculine and feminine forms of the numbers "two" (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">dau</i> and <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">dwy</i>), "three" (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">tri</i> and <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">tair</i>) and "four" (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">pedwar</i> and <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">pedair</i>), which must agree with the <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">grammatical gender</a> of the objects being counted. The objects being counted appear in the singular, not plural form.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>There is no standard or definitive form of the Welsh language.  Although northern and southern Welsh are two commonly mentioned main dialects, in reality additional significant variation exists within those areas. The more useful traditional classification refers to four main dialects: <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Y Wyndodeg</i>, the language of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Gwynedd" title="Kingdom of Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a>; <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Y Bowyseg</i>, the language of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Powys" title="Kingdom of Powys">Powys</a>; <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Y Ddyfedeg</i>, the language of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Dyfed" title="Kingdom of Dyfed">Dyfed</a>; and <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text"><a href="/wiki/Gwenhwyseg" title="Gwenhwyseg">Y Wenhwyseg</a></i>, the language of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Gwent" title="Kingdom of Gwent">Gwent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morgannwg" class="mw-redirect" title="Morgannwg">Morgannwg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Fine-grained classifications exist beyond those four: the book <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Cymraeg, Cymr\xc3\xa2g, Cymr\xc3\xaag: Cyflwyno\'r Tafodieithoedd</i> (Welsh&#160;for <i>Welsh, Welsh, Welsh: Introducing the Dialects</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> was accompanied by a cassette containing recordings of fourteen different speakers demonstrating aspects of different area dialects. The book also refers to the earlier <i>Linguistic Geography of Wales</i><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> as describing six different regions which could be identified as having words specific to those regions.\n</p><p>Another dialect is <a href="/wiki/Patagonian_Welsh" title="Patagonian Welsh">Patagonian Welsh</a>, which has developed since the start of <span lang="cy" style="font-style:normal;" title="Welsh language text"><a href="/wiki/Y_Wladfa" title="Y Wladfa">Y Wladfa</a></span> (the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_settlement_in_Argentina" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh settlement in Argentina">Welsh settlement in Argentina</a>) in 1865; it includes Spanish loanwords and terms for local features, but a survey in the 1970s showed that the language in Patagonia is consistent throughout the lower Chubut valley and in the Andes.\n</p><p>The differences in <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialect</a> are marked in pronunciation and in some vocabulary but also in minor points of grammar. For example: consider the question "Do you want a cuppa <i>[a cup of tea]</i>?" In Gwynedd this would typically be <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Dach chi isio panad?</i>  while in Glamorgan one would be more likely to hear <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Ych chi\'n moyn dishgled?</i> (though in other parts of the South one would not be surprised to hear <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Ych chi isie paned?</i> as well, among other possibilities). An example of a pronunciation difference is the tendency in some southern dialects to palatalise the letter "s", e.g. <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">mis</i> (Welsh&#160;for month), usually pronounced <small>IPA:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Welsh" title="Help:IPA/Welsh">[mi\xcb\x90s]</a></span>, but as <small>IPA:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Welsh" title="Help:IPA/Welsh">[mi\xcb\x90\xca\x83]</a></span> in parts of the south. This normally occurs next to a high front <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowel</a> like /i/, although exceptions include the pronunciation of <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">sut</i> "how" as <small>IPA:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Welsh" title="Help:IPA/Welsh">[\xca\x83\xca\x8ad]</a></span> in the southern dialects (compared with northern <small>IPA:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Welsh" title="Help:IPA/Welsh">[s\xc9\xa8t]</a></span>).\n</p><p>In the 1970s, there was an attempt to standardise the language by teaching <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Cymraeg Byw</i> ("Living Welsh") \xe2\x80\x93 a colloquially-based generic form of Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> But the attempt largely failed because it did not encompass the regional differences used by speakers of Welsh.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Registers">Registers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Registers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Modern Welsh can be considered to fall broadly into two main <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">registers</a>\xe2\x80\x94Colloquial Welsh (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Cymraeg llafar</i>) and Literary Welsh (<i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Cymraeg llenyddol</i>). The grammar described here is that of Colloquial Welsh, which is used in most speech and informal writing. Literary Welsh is closer to the form of Welsh standardised by the 1588 translation of the Bible and is found in official documents and other formal registers, including much literature. As a standardised form, literary Welsh shows little if any of the dialectal variation found in colloquial Welsh. Some differences include:\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Literary Welsh\n</th>\n<th>Colloquial Welsh\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Can omit subject pronouns (<a href="/wiki/Pro-drop_language" title="Pro-drop language">pro-drop</a>)\n</td>\n<td>Subject pronouns rarely omitted\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>More extensive use of simple verb forms\n</td>\n<td>More extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Periphrastic" class="mw-redirect" title="Periphrastic">periphrastic</a> verb forms\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>No distinction between simple present and future<br /> (e.g. <i>af</i> "I go"/"I shall go")\n</td>\n<td>Simple form most often expresses only future<br /> (e.g. <i>af i</i> "I\'ll go")\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Subjunctive verb forms\n</td>\n<td>Subjunctive in fixed idioms only\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>3rd.pl ending and pronoun <i>\xe2\x80\x93nt hwy</i>\n</td>\n<td>3rd.pl ending and pronoun <i>\xe2\x80\x93n nhw</i>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Amongst the characteristics of the literary, as against the spoken, language are a higher dependence on inflected verb forms, different usage of some of the tenses, less frequent use of pronouns (since the information is usually conveyed in the verb/preposition inflections) and a much lesser tendency to substitute English loanwords for native Welsh words. In addition, more archaic pronouns and forms of mutation may be observed in Literary Welsh.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples_of_sentences_in_literary_and_colloquial_Welsh">Examples of sentences in literary and colloquial Welsh</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Examples of sentences in literary and colloquial Welsh">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>English\n</th>\n<th>Literary Welsh\n</th>\n<th>Colloquial Welsh\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>I get up early every day.\n</td>\n<td><i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Codaf yn gynnar bob dydd</i>.\n</td>\n<td><i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Dw i\'n codi\'n gynnar bob dydd</i>. (North)<br /><i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Rwy\'n codi\'n gynnar bob dydd</i>.  (South)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>I\'ll get up early tomorrow.\n</td>\n<td><i>Codaf yn gynnar yfory.</i>\n</td>\n<td><i>Mi goda i\'n gynnar fory.</i>  (North)<br /><i>Wna i godi\'n gynnar fory.</i> (South)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>He had not stood there long.\n</td>\n<td><i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Ni safasai yno yn hir</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td><i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Doedd o ddim wedi sefyll yno\'n hir</i>. (North)<br /><i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">(D)\xc3\xb4dd e ddim wedi sefyll yna\'n hir</i>. (South)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>They\'ll sleep only when there\'s a need.\n</td>\n<td><i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Ni chysgant ond pan fo angen</i>.\n</td>\n<td><i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Fyddan nhw\'n cysgu ddim ond pan fydd angen</i>.\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In fact, the differences between dialects of modern spoken Welsh pale into insignificance compared to the difference between some forms of the spoken language and the most formal constructions of the literary language. The latter is considerably more conservative and is the language used in <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Bible" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Bible">Welsh translations</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, amongst other things - although the 2004 <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Beibl Cymraeg Newydd</i> (Welsh&#160;for <i>New Welsh Bible</i>) is significantly less formal than the traditional 1588 Bible.  Gareth King, author of a popular Welsh grammar, observes that "The difference between these two is much greater than between the virtually identical colloquial and literary forms of English".<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> A grammar of Literary Welsh can be found in <i>A Grammar of Welsh</i> by Stephen J. Williams<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> or more completely in <i>Gramadeg y Gymraeg</i> by Peter Wynn Thomas.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> (No comprehensive grammar of formal literary Welsh exists in English.)  An English-language guide to colloquial Welsh forms and register and dialect differences is <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Dweud Eich Dweud</i> by Ceri Jones.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="flag" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg/32px-Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png" width="32" height="19" class="noviewer thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg/48px-Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg/64px-Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="830" data-file-height="498" /></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Wales" title="Portal:Wales">Wales portal</a></span></li></ul></div>\n<div id="section_SpokenWikipedia" class="infobox sisterproject plainlinks haudio"><div style="text-align: center;
